B004DIPLMC|5.0|There is little question in my mind that the most glorious music qua music in Wagner's Ring cycle resides in the sumptuous orchestral 'accompaniment'. Indeed, like many I have enjoyed the orchestral portion of the Ring much more than I have the entire theatrical production itself. Others, like Stokowski, have felt the same way; Stoky made a condensed version of the Ring for orchestra alone [[ASIN:B000000WO0 Music From the Ring]]. Loren Maazel did, too, (at the behest of Wieland Wagner no less!) and that's what we get here with Maazel conducting the magnificent Berliner Philharmoniker, a live concert in 2000. (Actually the DVD is taken from TWO live performances from October 17 & 18, 2000.) One big difference between Stokowski's and Maazel's arrangements is that Maazel's is played straight through with no pauses, whereas Stoky's treats each section as complete in itself and with some additional music for endings and the like provided by Stokowski. Maazel has not added a single note of his own. Sometimes this makes for awkward transitions between sections, but this does not in itself ruin the whole. Further, Maazel has provided, in the booklet, a kind of synopsis for each section so that one has an idea of the action as one goes along.<br /><br />Of course Wagner was a genius of the orchestration and there are few orchestras in the world who can do his music as much justice as the Berliner Philharmoniker; their playing is simply marvelous, rich and voluptuous. One is struck, in this neatly filmed DVD, by how young many of the players of the BPO are and how much enjoyment they are getting from their own participation. Of course, by 2000 the orchestra had undergone massive personnel changes. It's amusing, for instance, to see the marvelous principal oboist, Albrecht Mayer, as a practically baby-faced very young man.<br /><br />One could wonder whether having a visual record of what is primarily an auditory experience, namely the performance of an orchestral work, is worth having. I submit that it is. This is an endlessly fascinating experience, or at least it was for me. But best of all, we don't have to deal with all those tiresome singers and all that bothersome stage action. And it only takes 75 minutes to get through the whole Ring!<br /><br />Scott Morrisonth having. I submit that it is. This is an endlessly fascinating experience, or at least it was for me. But best of all, we don't have to deal with all those tiresome singers and all that bothersome stage action. And it only takes 75 minutes to get through the whole Ring!<br /><br />Scott Morrison

They really out did themselves on this one; I know it's going to be a favorite of mine for years to come.

1935251740|3.0|On the plus side: Quantum physics reveals some very strange things about the very basic nature of `reality'. I think it was Niels Bohr who said something like \\"if quantum theory doesn't shock you, you haven't understood it\\". This book does a very good job of explaining some of these strange results, including detailed descriptions of the experiments used to confirm the `shocking' theoretical propositions. The book is also very interesting early on when the authors discuss the limits of science. The book is worth buying on these fronts alone.    However, I only gave the book 3 stars because of it has shortcomings. First, I couldn't quite follow some of the logical leaps the authors made. For example, one of the early critical foundational arguments is that a tree falling in the forest doesn't make a sound if no one is there because the tree only makes vibrations in the air, but sound is a cognition so if nobody is there, there is no `sound'.  I wasn't very convinced by this argument, nor by many of the other segues into the 7 principles of Biocentrism. I think they also stretch quantum physics a bit too far at times. Thus, in the end, the book failed to convince me of its central argument (which, despite the authors' claims, seemed a trite bit `new-agey' to me).    Overall, there is some merit to the book, and I would give it a higher recommendation if the authors cut its length in half by getting to the point quicker and cutting out some of the protracted stories about personal experiences.

B0049VGTGA|5.0|Genesis singer and drummer Phil Collins' first proper solo album Face Value was released in February of 1981.<br />Phil was the last member of Genesis to do a proper solo album (the Brand X albums were not real solo albums but an outside project for Phil when Genesis was not recording). When Face Value hit the streets, people were in awe over the excellent production, Phil co-produced with Hugh Padgham, and engineering of Hugh Padgham and listeners were immediately aware that this album was a collection of letters to his first wife whom left him during Genesis' And Then There Were Three Tour in 1978 and would divorce in 1979.<br />Also, the Face Value album has a collage of musical styles (running the gamut from Genesis' atmospheric music to Motown to jazz to funk to soft rock to regular rock to art rock).<br />The opening US Top 20 hit (which was the second single here in the States from the album) \\"In the Air Tonight\\" was a classic that used the metaphor of a drowning man as the demise of the divorce of his first marriage. The track, like all the others features Phil's superb vocals and drumming but also showed he could play keyboards and write great songs. He wrote ten of the album's twelve tracks himself. Also, Genesis touring guitarist/bass player Daryl Steurmer's guitar here is phenomenal. \\"I Missed Again\\" was the album's other US Top 20 hit (and was the first US single) and is another highlight featuring the Earth Wind and Fire horn section and famed British sax player Ronnie Scott giving this track a Motown feel. \\"This Must Be Love\\" is an optimistic song about Phil rebounding from the demise of his first marriage to find peace with a new love in his life (whom would be his second wife Jill). \\"The Roof is Leaking\\" is a great country blues number with some killer slide guitar from Joe Partridge (interesting that Eric Clapton did play on an earlier take with the dobro but was scrapped) and banjo from Daryl.<br />The two instrumentals \\"Droned\\" and \\"Hand in Hand\\" are superb as well (the latter is his most well known instrumental). My favorite track on the album is the ballad \\"If Leaving Me is Easy\\" which is a very sad song and will bring a tear to your eye after listening to the lyrics and the melody and orchestrations on the track. If this doesn't do the trick then you either are insensitive or a robot.<br />The Genesis track \\"Behind the Lines\\" was re-done as a disco/funk tune after Phil played the original recording back at double speed during the Duke sessions in 1979 and a new version was born complete with horns. His cover of The Beatles' \\"Tomorrow Never Knows\\" (the album's closer) is the best cover version of the song I ever heard and a great album closer. He slowed the tempo down and made this song as it was his own. Ironically, the track turned into a tribute to John Lennon who passed away two months before this album was released. Then this is Phil's only album with a hidden track of him singing \\"Over the Rainbow\\" at the tail end of \\"Tomorrow Never Knows\\".<br />The other tracks the sweet piano/orchestra ballad \\"You Know What I Mean\\", \\"Thunder and Lightning\\" and \\"I'm Not Moving\\" (with Phil on all instruments save John Giblin's bass guitar) are great songs as well but people overlook these.<br />When released, the Face Value album shot straight to #7 here in the US (outcharting every previous Genesis related release up to that point) and sold four million copies (which was more than any Genesis album up to that point) here in the United States alone.<br />I first discovered this album when my dad had the cassette when I was a child. Then, in late August of 1997 I bought the CD and played this album constantly and was the soundtrack to the fall semester of college for me that year.<br />In 1994, Atlantic Records released a limited run Gold CD of this album which is now LONG OUT OF PRINT. For those who want a Gold CD of this album, I recommend you buying this. The mastering on this new CD is much superior to the original 1980s CD issue. Most re-issues out now of albums are compressed to sound appealing to the MP3/iPod loving sheep (which I do not apply as I don't own either an MP3 player nor an iPod). If people are waiting for a \\"COMPRESSED TO HELL\\" remaster will have to keep on waiting but those who want a Gold CD issue with excellent sonic quality and also all the original artwork (save for the gatefold pictures but does have photos of original vinyl LP Atlantic labels) and with lyrics and some misspellings of credits fixed, go for it!<br />Highly recommended!t now of albums are compressed to sound appealing to the MP3/iPod loving sheep (which I do not apply as I don't own either an MP3 player nor an iPod). Highly recommended!

This is still a good album and the more I listen to it the more I like it. It's not nearly as fun or memorable as their previous work. This is one of those cases where you wonder if the band is happy with the new songs or if they were pushed in a certain direction by producers?    Looking forward to future material from White Lies.

B004APVI1G|4.0|The Legend of the Guardians is a strange tale- and I'm not even talking about the actual film.  Hyped during its theatrical run (which began on September 24th of 2010), Warner Brother announced that it was scheduled for a mid-February DVD/ Blu-ray release.  Then suddenly (presumably in effort to capitalize on the spend-happy Holiday shopper scene), the DVD & Blu-ray were released and with virtually no hoopla on December 17th.  I nearly completely overlooked it as a result except for the fact that when preordering Despicable Me, I was recommended Guardians with the sub text \\"available now\\".<br /><br />Going into the film, I was aware of the fact that the material would be joining an ever-growing list (a list that includes Shrek, Meet the Robinsons, How to Train your Dragon, Cloudy with a Chance of Meatballs to mention a few) of animated features based on popular children's book series.  This one was done up by the studio responsible for Happy Feet (Village Roadshow), distributed by powerhouse Warner Brothers and represents Zack Snyder's (the man responsible for such works as Watchmen and 300) directorial debut in animated feature film.<br /><br />On the whole, Warner Brothers has been lagging behind in the highly lucrative animated feature market; a place dominated by Disney/ Pixar and DreamWorks.  Other studios in similar positions have begun to realize the potential in this segment and are putting in serious work to catch up (Sony with ImageWorks, 20th Century Fox with Blue Sky and so on).  WB hasn't proven quite as eager as the competition so converting Kathryn Lasky's series of young reader fantasy books into CG is about as good a place to start making up for lost time.<br /><br />Legend of the Guardians: The Owls of Ga'Hoole' uses personified owls to weave its tale in a completely fantastical world (seemingly devoid of humans).  The plot works on the fantasy template on nearly a point-by-point basis:  A young, inexperienced (eventual) hero is introduced from the safety of his picturesque home.  Innocence and security are whisked away by invasion of a powerful enemy army, complete with a weapon of incalculable advantage.  The only force in the world capable of keeping the rising threat at bay have long since retired to the recesses of memory and myth.<br /><br />The quest begins to not only prove the legend true, but to convince the Guardians to come out of their self-imposed retirement to once again rise up against this evil empire (The Pure Ones) to restore peace and harmony to the whole land.  Along the way there will be requisite betrayals, family division, advice from goodhearted heroes, training from hardened warriors, a better understanding of the horrors of war and why freedom is worth fighting to preserve.<br /><br />If this all sounds familiar, that's because it's a story archetype that's been told countless times throughout the ages.  Lord of the Rings, Star Wars, Eragon; you can find this precise tale in various forms of entertainment spanning literature, film, poetry, folklore and so on.  However, don't be mistaken into believing that because its formulaic, Legend of the Guardians comes up short.  But we'll get to that.<br /><br />Notice the apparent lack of names included in my above story summary?  That was a deliberate homage to the actual experience in that upon the film's conclusion, I was confident only in the names of two of the fifteen main characters.<br /><br />Names like Otulissa, Strix Struma, Ezylryb, Eglantine and Allomere were made clear to me only in post-viewing research and even then I was quite unsure which were which. Australian accents and little scripting effort to embed faces with the names results is a definite \\"so that's the main guy's brother there?\\" experience.  Fortunately, somehow, the film suffers none as a result of this little snafu.  A marketing disaster, sure, but the entertainment value endures.<br /><br />And speaking of entertainment value, Legend of the Guardians is a visual feast! It can truly be said that Pixar and DreamWorks have absolutely nothing on Village Roadshow.  Lighting is spectacular, settings are rich with background detail and the character models are so well textured/ animated that they often border on photorealism.  The flight sequences, particularly when viewed in high-definition, are breathtaking.  I can only imagine what the experience must have been like in a 3D theater.<br /><br />There are a few complaints to be voiced however.  The weapon of the enemy (an energy field that somehow captures owls and renders them helpless) is kept a complete mystery to the viewer for the duration of the film.  Whether or not it is better explained in the source material books, I cannot say, but aside from requiring tiny little pieces of metal to function, it is nothing more than a one-dimensional plot device here.<br /><br />Also the PG rating should be taken quite seriously as, though animated, this film contains some pretty intense sequences that will surely unsettle the youngsters.  Additionally, even if the kid in question is desensitized to such things, the plot and themes would likely be a bit heavy/ hard to follow for younger tykes.<br /><br />In all though, despite what surely sounds like multiple complaints, Legend of the Guardians: The Owls of Ga'Hoole' is a surprisingly solid piece of computer generated film.  The story is a bit clich&eacute; yet it still manages to engross, the character and location names are a venerable mystery, yet the story manages to shine through, the 3D revolution may be a fad but the visuals here simply refuse to stop dazzling.  At the end of the day, the movie is 97 minutes of sheer enjoyable adventure: a classic tale of good versus evil, told with animation of absolutely epic proportions.  The fantasy element is quite strong throughout and offers fans of films like the Harry Potter series, Lord of the Rings and Star Wars a fully CG feature to relish.olutely nothing on Village Roadshow.  Lighting is spectacular, settings are rich with background detail and the character models are so well textured/ animated that they often border on photorealism.  The flight sequences, particularly when viewed in high-definition, are breathtaking.  I can only imagine what the experience must have been like in a 3D theater.    There are a few complaints to be voiced however.  The weapon of the enemy (an energy field that somehow captures owls and renders them helpless) is kept a complete mystery to the viewer for the duration of the film.  Whether or not it is better explained in the source material books, I cannot say, but aside from requiring tiny little pieces of metal to function, it is nothing more than a one-dimensional plot device here.    Also the PG rating should be taken quite seriously as, though animated, this film contains some pretty intense sequences that will surely unsettle the youngsters.  Additionally, even if the kid in question is desensitized to such things, the plot and themes would likely be a bit heavy/ hard to follow for younger tykes.    In all though, despite what surely sounds like multiple complaints, Legend of the Guardians: The Owls of Ga'Hoole' is a surprisingly solid piece of computer generated film.  The story is a bit clich yet it still manages to engross, the character and location names are a venerable mystery, yet the story manages to shine through, the 3D revolution may be a fad but the visuals here simply refuse to stop dazzling.  At the end of the day, the movie is 97 minutes of sheer enjoyable adventure: a classic tale of good versus evil, told with animation of absolutely epic proportions.  The fantasy element is quite strong throughout and offers fans of films like the Harry Potter series, Lord of the Rings and Star Wars a fully CG feature to relish.

We can probably all relate to someone that we once had a deep connection with in our younger years who we eventually drifted apart from, but always felt a connection to. There are times where it feels as if she is trying to hard to impress the reader with an overly creative description of what should be a simple matter. Ultimately my complaints about this book are minor and really reflect the fact that she and Robert were human and imperfect. It is a really personal story and very well told. I enjoyed it.

Maybe I'm just getting older and more nostalgic...who knows.<br /><br />On a side note, I find it interesting that a lot of the Amazon reviewers complain about the production of this album and then in the same breath praise the low-budget poorly produced 80's albums like Hell Awaits, Reign in Blood, and Show No Mercy.  Doesn't make any sense.<br /><br />Finally, from reading the reviews here, it's obvious that Slayer fans are a diverse lot that possess many varying views on what they like about the band's direction and what they don't like about it.  To me, World Painted Blood is pretty much a standard 21st century Slayer release.  Not bad, but then again, not outstanding either...there are a handful of memorable tracks on here that will stand the test of time.  I give this album 3+ stars.  I want to give it 4, and maybe it will grow on me and obtain that 4th star one day, but it's not quite there yet...

I always like to think that there are 8 essential Motorhead albums from throughout their career. Albums that truly represent the cream of their studio output. These albums also conveniently cover all the line ups which itself is testament to the band's quality. They are Overkill, Ace of Spades, Another Perfect Day, Orgasmatron, 1916, Bastards, Overnight Sensation and Inferno. The World is Yours is getting added to that list.

While this is his most technologically impressive and aspiring film it is not his best. In fact, it is the worst of his catalog but that isn't to say it is bad, it's just not as incredible as his other films. No&eacute; has stated this film was inspired by psychedelic experiences and the \\"Tibetan Book of The Dead\\" which discusses the death experience including re-birth. He even traveled to the Amazon to experience one of the most powerful psychedelics known, \\"Ayahuasca\\". No&eacute; laughingly stated this was for \\"professional research\\".<br /><br />Newcomer Nathanial Brown plays \\"Oscar\\", an American 20 something living in Tokyo trying to make ends meet by selling drugs. Him and his sister \\"Linda\\" played by a sultry Paz De la Huerta are orphans from a horrific car crash when they were children. Oscar has a slight obsession with breasts, psychedelic drugs, and inappropriate relationships; including one with his sister. The story is told in three parts, first showing Oscar's point of view as he takes DMT and deals drugs. Second, we are in the POV of Oscar's soul after being shot, re-living the events leading up to his demise. In this section, we learn that he has managed to move his sister out to Tokyo where she has becomes a stripper. We witness the car crash that took their parents and learn about his \\"awkward\\" relationship with Linda. Third, we see Oscar's soul traveling the city watching over his sister as he promised her that he will \\"never leave her\\". The ending is ambiguous, but most of his films are; though for the first time that I know of, he revealed in a few interviews big hints as to what really happens. So be careful if you are reading interviews prior to watching the film.<br /><br />As with most of No&eacute;'s films, ETV attempts to alter the viewer's perception through the use of sound and visuals. \\"I Stand Alone\\" and \\"Irreversible\\" were both filmed in this way and both managed to completely engross me into the characters psyche. In \\"Enter The Void\\", No&eacute; attempts this right off the bat in the opening credit sequence. He uses an industrial trance song from the England techno group \\"LFO\\". Partnered with pulsating lights and text, he essential brings the audience into a psychedelic \\"trip\\". Some will be immediately turned off by this beginning sequence as it is almost seizure inducing but those who stick it out are in for a treat. The soundtrack was an integral part as well. There was music, but mostly it was background noise and what I think was Binaural Tones. Binaural Tones or Beats are low frequency sounds meant to alter the brain waves in an effort to change perception. If there is any truth to this phenomenon, I think the audience experiences it first hand while watching.<br /><br />Noe uses the same technique as he did in \\"Irreversible\\" having the camera float above and transition through solid objects. This was one of the few things I didn't like about the movie. I felt like I had seen it before and that he wasn't presenting anything new. The floating aside, he did managed to show me something I have never seen before. The POV technique. I couldn't believe what I was watching when Oscar was standing in the mirror washing his face. The camera blacked out perfectly in sync with when Oscar puts his hands over his eyes. It was fascinating to watch, in fact there are many camera shots in the film that left my jaw dropped.<br /><br />The script was great but some of the performances were not. I was surprised by this since all of his previous films were incredibly acted. No&eacute; stated in an interview that there was a lot of improvisation on set in regards to the script. I can't help but think maybe this had something to do withthe poor acting. I suppose the improvisation helped the film seem more real, but it just felt forced. One standout for me was the actress who plays the younger version of Linda. Emily Alyn Lind sent chills down my spine with her portrayal of a child witnessing her parents horrific death.<br /><br />Another surprising, but welcome change is that this movie wasn't very violent. Pretty much all of No&eacute;'s previous feature films scared the hell out of me. This trip was different, though he still featured graphic sex as he tends to do in all of his films. But this movie isn't about sex and violence. At it's heart it is about the unknown (death) and shows the collective experience of many people over many centuries (psychedelics). Early in the film Oscars friend \\"Alex\\", played by Cyril Roy, mentions that the chemical in with DMT and Ayahuasca is in the human body already. He says that when you die the DMT is released essentially giving you the \\"hardest trip\\" you can get. My theory is that Noe wanted to show the effect of taking DMT recreationally (Oscar's first person view of a DMT trip early in the film) and the effect of DMT naturally occurring at the moment of death. A brilliant concept that he pulled off flawlessly.<br /><br />As with most of his films, this is not for everyone. You have to be able to appreciate the craft of movie making to really understand the technical accomplishment this film was. Some critics have said this is \\"style over substance\\", but I disagree. The film has its faults, yes, but it is enjoyable and captivating. I've never seen anything like it and honestly probably never will. Gaspar No&eacute; is one of the best directors in the world today, I can't wait to see what he thinks up next.<br /><br />[...]l, but it just felt forced. B00009W0U4|4.0|One of my favorite directors of all time and one of my favorite movies. \\"Irreversible\\" is an experience unlike any other. Gaspar Noe uses reverse-chronological story telling to give us one of the most visceral and frightening encounters you are ever likely to have in the Cinema. The film is beautifully shot and intensely acted. \\"Irreversible\\" is Noe's first use of the floating camera technique that he also used in the 2009 film \\"Enter The Void\\", another roller-coaster of a film. Not surprisingly, this film is not for everyone. It is widely known as one of the most difficult movies to watch due to the graphic violence and long rape scene so be warned. But it is not just a shock film, it has an important message about relationships and how the decisions we make ultimately decide our fate.<br /><br />The story involves a couple played by Monica Bellucci and Vincent Cassel who after a heated argument split ways for the evening. Alex, played by Bellucci, gets fed up with her boyfriend Marcus (Cassel) and leaves the safety of her friends to go home. A decision we eventually find out is a near fatal one. Marcus finds out that his love has been brutally raped and beaten on her way home. This sends him into a rage of revenge and he will not stop until he finds the one responsible. Along the way Marcus' best friend and Alex's former lover tries to convince him to leave it be so he won't do something he will regret. As you can imagine, he is unsuccessful.<br /><br />\\"Irreversible\\" starts at the end of the story and ends at the beginning, much like the movie \\"Memento\\" by Christopher Nolan. A decision that could have gone either way. Most people like the reverse story telling, I think it could have gone either way. Gaspar Noe is known to push the limits and make his audience as uncomfortable as he can by graphically presenting topics that are hard to witness and think about. In this outing he decides to hit you with the heaviest scenes in the beginning and middle of the movie then ending with the character development. Thankfully, the third act was designed to slowly bring the audience back from the horror they have just seen. By the end you have (slightly) come to terms with the earlier terror and you are left with an array of emotions.<br /><br />The production team Noe surrounds himself with are some of the best in the business. \\"Irreversible\\" is a marvel of film making using dreary soundtracks and showcasing the seedy underbelly of France. As mentioned before the use of the floating camera is outstanding; a major challenge since it was filmed using cables suspended in the air. It's as if you are on a ride, one that you want to get off but can't; the ultimate \\"fly on the wall\\" experience.<br /><br />\\"Irreversible\\" was filmed with a loose script and literally no dialogue. In fact, the infamous rape scene was essentially free-form by Bellucci and La Tenia, the antagonist played by Jo Prestia. Gaspar used one long take from a fixed camera to film this scene which made a huge impact. It caused the viewer to believe they are witnessing an actual rape on camera. It truly is the most difficult scene I have ever witnessed. Many people walked out due to this scene during the premier, cursing the director. Noe, however, loves to get these reactions as it shows his goal to push the boundaries was successful. Cassel and Bellucci have amazing chemistry (it helps they are actually married). Bellucci is as beautiful as ever and Cassel presents an intensity rarely seen from actors. I think having his wife presented with such a horrific experience pushed him further. Pretty much all of the other characters were cast with unknowns or people that haven't acted at all which pushed the realism.<br /><br />It has been over a year since my first viewing of this film and I still think about it. \\"Irreversible\\" is not a film that can easily be watched over and over and probably shouldn't be. Afterward you feel drained, angry, sad and confused. Confused because you aren't sure whether you just watched one of the worst or best films ever made. If you are up for the challenge, I highly recommend giving this a shot, but be warned it's not for the faint of heart.<br /><br />[...]one of the worst or best films ever made. B004927I50|3.0|The evolution of country music into an alternate genre where its songs get radio-play has often been ridiculed - after all, we've had such pop-candy stalwarts such as Shania Twain, Carrie Underwood, and even Faith Hill to cite as examples. But Taylor Swift is a definitive example of the broadening of country music - to include influences such as rock and bluegrass, so much so that the `country' aspect of it is thoroughly diluted. It is with this palette that Swift has created \\"Speak Now\\", a decidedly inferior followup to her breakthrough album \\"Fearless\\", only because it's a very obvious sellout (much in the vein of Shania Twains \\"Up\\", but at least on that CD Shania had the decency to release separate albums for the pop, world, and country audiences - a feat that's never been replicated since).<br /><br />While \\"Fearless\\" wore its heart on its sleeve in an off-handed manner, \\"Speak Now\\" is more pointed and unnecessarily vicious. What seemed like innocent schoolgirl rants on \\"Fearless\\" have evolved into worrisome, even deranged complaints by a woman who is now well in her twenties. Prime amongst these is \\"Dear John\\" about John Mayer. Granted, I have no real fondness for Mayer either as a musician or as a personality of any sort, but how anyone could deserve such an accusatory track dedicated to them is beyond me. Ironically, this is the best track on the entire album - from a musical perspective - and if you can ignore the `woe is me' lyrics, there is much to enjoy here (let us pretend not to notice the absolute lack of chord progression on this song, but then again, any musician will tell you that all of Swifts songs are written in the `key of flat', so no surprises there).<br /><br />The innate issue with an album of this sort is that you get into it expecting more of the same that you saw on her earlier work, but the sheen has worn off, and the results aren't exactly pretty. Lead single \\"Mine\\", which seemed to me a stellar first single especially due to its lyrics on the chorus, ironically stalled at No. 2 on the Country Charts and didn't give Taylor the No. 1 Billboard Country Songs ribbon that she'd wanted. It was widely expected that this song would do that, but it didn't. Second single \\"Back to December\\" is interesting only because Swift appears to be taking the blame on this (rather than constantly blaming everyone else, which, lets be honest, is her musical forte!), but the verse-chorus-verse here is rather listenable and you might find yourself singing along to this on the radio. However, for every listenable track here, there are tons of rather poorly constructed and ill-performed numbers that go literally nowhere. \\"Mean\\" for example is meant to address a particular media journalist but ends up as rather tacky and in sad taste. The same can be said of the faux-rock mood of \\"The Story of Us\\" which was a very weak melody to begin with. These problems were not apparent on \\"Fearless\\" where even random album tracks such as \\"Youre not sorry\\" could have been potential hit singles.<br /><br />The other issue is that \\"Speak Now\\" is front-loaded with all its potential hits and the `big songs', leading to the album falling to pieces in the second half. This is unfortunate because they were really onto something with the first half, at least in terms of scoring some radio airplay or replays on CD. I cant imagine anyone wanting to listen to \\"Innocent\\" (her very shoddy critique of the entire Kanye West episode) more than once, and \\"Never Grow Up\\" is just too easy. If you were expecting Swift to bring her A game and revolutionize country music, this is not the album you should be looking at. A quick glance at some other reviews will all give added points to packaging rather than quality. For example, one reviewer calls this the `best packaged CD of the last ten years' because of the cover art, inclusion of lyrics, and superior artwork. Point noted, but honestly, aren't we supposed to judge a music CD based on the actual music rather than the packaging? Or is that the state of current country music where an album cover makes the CD get an extra star? The last two songs on this album, especially \\"Long Live\\", are very much culled from demos and quickly recorded - and the impact they make is just as temporary and fleeting.<br /><br />The Deluxe Edition of this CD gets you six bonus tracks (3 new songs, two acoustic versions and a remix). \\"Superman\\", while interesting, should have made the lead edition of the album instead of the Deluxe as its obviously leaps and bounds over anything else on the Deluxe bonus CD. However, if you try comparing this to \\"Fearless\\", you will find that the deluxe edition is nowhere as great as that one (remember it contained tons of videos on the bonus DVD as well as some killer bonus tracks). Still, I suppose the effort is appreciated. \\"Speak Now\\" debuted at No. 1 on the Billboard 200 Albums Chart, and broke records as it sold a million copies in the first week. Sales-wise, that is extremely impressive, but its not quite deserved, I think. There are ideas here, thoughts with some backbone, but their delivery is just not up to the mark, and while Taylor as a vocalist has definitely made strides (check out her crystal clear voice on \\"Dear John\\", which remains the album highlight), she needs to record better material. Also, this album would have been a whole lot better had they paid more attention to the entire second half of the record, which to me is virtually unlistenable. For those of you who think that comparisons to \\"Fearless\\" are uncalled for, well, it did win Album of the Year at the Grammys, so that's saying something, and does raise the benchmark pretty high. \\"Speak Now\\" just can't reach those heights though.<br /><br />Taylor Swift remains a reasonably talented singer (though not a country singer), and I must say that if I had to compare her two other contemporary country singers - Carrie Underwood & Miranda Lambert, she comes across as third on the list. Carrie's last album \\"Play On\\" is definitely notches better than \\"Speak Now\\" (hey, at least it was a `country' album), but Miranda Lambert currently rules the country roost for me with 2010's spectacular \\"Revolution\\". Comparing that exceptional album to Swifts new one is like comparing Chateaubriand steak to a Kit-Kat. Swift is out of her league on that one. But, shes still young and has a decent voice, so lets hope she finally records a proper country album some day (like perhaps \\"Theres More Where That Came From\\" by Lee Ann Womack. Now that's country!)<br /><br />Three Stars. An average effort at best; and if you strip away the media-machine and PR, the music just doesn't cut it.is that the state of current country music where an album cover makes the CD get an extra star? The last two songs on this album, especially \\"Long Live\\", are very much culled from demos and quickly recorded - and the impact they make is just as temporary and fleeting. However, if you try comparing this to \\"Fearless\\", you will find that the deluxe edition is nowhere as great as that one (remember it contained tons of videos on the bonus DVD as well as some killer bonus tracks). Still, I suppose the effort is appreciated. \\"Speak Now\\" debuted at No. 1 on the Billboard 200 Albums Chart, and broke records as it sold a million copies in the first week. Sales-wise, that is extremely impressive, but its not quite deserved, I think. There are ideas here, thoughts with some backbone, but their delivery is just not up to the mark, and while Taylor as a vocalist has definitely made strides (check out her crystal clear voice on \\"Dear John\\", which remains the album highlight), she needs to record better material. Also, this album would have been a whole lot better had they paid more attention to the entire second half of the record, which to me is virtually unlistenable. For those of you who think that comparisons to \\"Fearless\\" are uncalled for, well, it did win Album of the Year at the Grammys, so that's saying something, and does raise the benchmark pretty high. \\"Speak Now\\" just can't reach those heights though. Now that's country!)    Three Stars. An average effort at best; and if you strip away the media-machine and PR, the music just doesn't cut it.

There's nothing about this that strikes the reader as Happily Ever After and perhaps there's a good reason for that. There is a spin off series to come out that's tied to this world.  There are still plenty of things that need resolution, and although we knew Mackayla is epic, she can't or rather shouldn't do anything with that power.  So in terms of what happens in Shadowever, you won't be disappointed.  In terms of how it happens, you would probably have a few things you would change.

0765326353|4.0|During his teenage years, when Brandon Sanderson was in college working on his writing and looking to make it as an author, in the back of his mind a story idea formed and began to germinate and develop and become more and more complex.  Over a decade later, with Sanderson firmly established as one of the most important, bestselling fantasy writers being published today, he has now turned his story idea that was a dream into a reality in The Way of Kings, the first book of the Stormlight Archive.    Epic fantasy story aside, the book itself is a work of art: beautiful maps on the inside covers, further maps and illustrations and drawings throughout the lengthy book as relates to the story, illustrations for each chapter title, and a captivating cover by artist Michael Whelan.  Another part of the dream come true for Sanderson is the allowance of the publisher Tor to publish a book weighing in at 1008 pages; it indicates Tor has full confidence in the book and in Sanderson.    Sanderson begins the book with description into the important history of this world.  Long ago there was a mighty war between the Voidbringers and the knights known as Radiants who used a special kind of armor known as Shardplates and their weapons were the unstoppable Shardblades - \\"A Shardblade did not cut flesh; it severed the soul itself\\" - but then the Radiants turned against mankind and abandoned them, leaving their armor and weapons.  We turn to the present day, thousands of years later to the Shattered Plains: a harsh and horrific landscape where armies battle the enemy for domination, with the goal of securing more Shardplates and Shardblades.  Then there are the Chasmfiends, great, destructive, terrifying beasts that they also fight to kill, for deep within their crustacean bodies lie the priceless gemhearts.    Our story focuses on two characters.  One is Kaladin, a young man in his twenties who has seen much of life already.   Raised by his surgeon father to become a brilliant doctor, he instead turns to the life of a warrior, with hopes of getting his hands on a Shardblade, and soon sees his fair share of death and bloodshed.  Now he is a slave, for reasons unknown, with little to hope for in life.  He soon becomes a member of the bridge crew, a group of slaves whose job it is to carry a giant, heavy bridge across great distances and to lay it across the chasms to allow the soldiers to cross and attack the enemy.  Kaladin becomes part of bridge team four, which is renowned for losing the most lives each time it races into battle.  Kaladin finds a unique luck on his side, as he manages to continue to survive, and then chooses to work for his team, train them, create survival tactics for them, and he discovers something he thought he'd lost for good: hope and his will to live.    Then there is Shallan, a young woman whose family has fallen on hard times after the death of their father.  The family is in possession of a Soulcaster, a unique magical device that can essentially create just about anything out of nothing, only now it is broken.  However, Shallan has a plan: to become the ward and student for Jasnah Kolin, sister of King Elhokar of Althekar, with plans to replace Jasnah's Soulcaster with her own; her only problem is she has no idea how to use it.    While a thousand pages may have been a little too much for Sanderson to tell the stories he wanted to tell in this first volume of the projected ten-book series, as some story lines drag a little before something happens, Sanderson has nevertheless done what he does best: created a truly unique fantasy world that at times feels as complete and complex as our own.  There is the class system of eye color with darkeyes, who are looked down upon, and lighteyes who are the only ones who may bear Shardblades (though there are hints that this is not set in stone).  There is the important religion of the Vorin which most of the known world follows, which tells of the struggle between the Voidbringers and humanity; while some other religions are hinted at; and then there is Jasnah Kolin, an atheist and decreed a heretic, who is a most unique and fascinating character.  A number of interludes throughout the book help to introduce some minor characters to explore some more of this overwhelming world, such as Szeth-son-son-Vallano, who is an assassin from the land of Shinovar, possessing a unique magic to flip gravity around.  And then there are the spren, which are spirits that seem to be caused by or drawn to specific happenstances and emotions, such as fear, pain, music, rot, and glory to name a few.  Little is known or understood of the spren, other than they exist, while Kaladin finds himself befriending a specific spren that seems to be evolving.    One would think that much could be told in a thousand pages, but Sanderson has literally barely chipped slivers off the mighty iceberg of The Stormlight Archive, but considering he has the penultimate Wheel of Time book coming out in November at almost 900 pages, Sanderson has certainly proven that he can get a lot of good quality writing done when he needs to, so fans shouldn't have to wait too long before the next mighty volume in this terrific new series is released.    Originally written on October 7 2010 Alex C. Telander.    Go to BookBanter ([...]) for over five hundred reviews and over forty exclusive author interviews, and more.

0553805371|3.0|As a reader, reviewer, and writer, I often wonder how word of mouth and rumor and the great press machine get rolling.  Take The Grand Design for example: Stephen Hawking has been wowing the world since A Brief History of Time, and with the publication of his latest book approaching, commentaries and thoughts began to spread on how this book would challenge the fundamental juxtaposition of science vs. religion once and for all.  After reading the under-200-page book of theories, discussions, photos and diagrams, my conclusion on The Grand Design is that for the most part it reads like a short college textbook on particle physics and the universe.  Nothing necessarily revolutionary or shocking in its thoughts and ideas that couldn't be found in a number of other science books; perhaps the key is the important author name on the cover?    While The Grand Design isn't the explosive book word of mouth has made it up to be (however it has sold considerably well) - akin to Colin Tudge's The Link: Uncovering Our Earliest Ancestor which was supposed to revolutionize the field of archaeology - it does nevertheless possess some interesting and at times fascinating theories and discussions of why things are the way they are.  For the last couple of decades physicists and astronomical scientists have been doing their day to day jobs, while in the back of their minds they have been hoping to stumble upon the unification theory, essentially - as Douglas Adams precisely put it - a theory of life, the universe and everything.  From a physics standpoint, it is the bringing together of gravity, Einstein's relativity theory, and the theory of quantum mechanics which will help explain why everything is the way it is and how it got to being there.  In the Grand Design, Hawking, along with co-author Leonard Mlodinow, give readers a strong introduction on the universe, particle physics, quantum mechanics and gravitational forces, and the launch into M Theory - the theory of life, the universe and everything.  While they don't have all the answers yet, and there's still a lot of ground to cover, it seems that M Theory may in fact be that ideal that scientists have been searching for for so long. Ms. Shriver has an excellent story to tell, and at times it is well told and even gripping.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the very style of the story (letters describing events to a person who was there) was a draw back. I suppose this was to set up for surprises later in the book, but it simply didn't work.<br /><br />The story of Eva's relationship with her husband and son would have made for interesting reading, but it was so hard to get past the fact that I was reading a novel, due to the self-important (and unrealistic) style and language. This is \\"a novel\\", and the reader is not going to forget it. There were times, though, that the story was interesting enough for me to over look this (hence the 2 stars instead of 1), but those instances were few.<br /><br />In addition, We Need to Talk About Kevin is simply too long. Ms. Shriver spends too much time on details and issues that don't add to the story and that could easily have been pared. Other school shooting incidents discussed in detail, the 2000 election fiasco in Florida referred to again and again, feelings examined in minutiae. . . This book weighs in at 400 pages in oversized paperback, and would probably have been a good novel if 1/4 of that had been left out.<br /><br />Another difficulty I had with We Need to Talk About Kevin was the discussions (generally arguments) between Eva and Franklin (recounted in detail by Eva to Franklin despite the fact he was there) about their son. These conversations were not realistic, read like how a young person might imagine adults talk, and certainly did not read like adults talking about their own children. Perhaps Ms. Shriver intended this, used it show the difficulty between Eva and Franklin. Perhaps, but to this reader it did have any purposeful use, and made reading even more difficult.<br /><br />Due to the over-scrutiny, the self-importance and the length, by the time the book ended, the \\"surprise\\" was no surprise and the ending was simply a relief. If Ms. Shriver had kept with just the basic story, and had Eva give it in a different format, this could have been a stellar read. As it is, I advise you give it a miss.he was there) about their son. 0525423842|5.0|John Grisham is a distinguished writer of popular fiction.  I have read many of his books and all of them have fascinated and delighted me. Most of his novels pertain to court cases, laws, and the overall judicial system.  He vividly describes the court atmosphere. His delineation of the general character and peculiar behavior of the lawyers, judges, and clerks is not only realistic, but also amusing at times. The lawyer mother of 13- year old Theo Boones is described as trying to lose weight for the past ten years and, therefore, she takes only coffee for breakfast. I was amused at her efforts and reminded of my own struggle with the same problem.    The story concerns with one Mr. Duffy indicted for murdering his wife but without any eyewitness to prove the case. Mr. Grisham then proceeds to bring a lot of life to the characters by describing their attire, their mood, their surroundings, their style of conversation and mannerisms. If a lawyer is having coffee and doughnut, you can feel the taste of the fresh, warm coffee and the sweetness of the crisp doughnut.  The author describes the settings in such detail that you feel as if you have visited the place yourself. He uses his pen as a paintbrush to draw pictures of such intensity that the reader is swept off his feet.  The principal character in the novel, a 13 years old lad named Theodore Boone acts like a lawyer.  He is ambitious and wants to become a trial lawyer. Both of his parents are lawyers. That makes it easy for him to go to the court often after school and loiter around the place.  Since he has been in the court often, he is able to learn and absorb the rules and regulations of the court and vocabulary of the law.  The protagonist of the novel aims to teach some terminology to other kids of his age by making them understand issues related to court. For example, he explains the meaning of words like, mortgage, interest rate, bank foreclosure, bankruptcy, circumstantial evidence, immunity, animal control, prosecutor, defense lawyer, direct evidence, indirect evidence, and the phrase \\"proven beyond the shadow of doubt.\\"  The explanations are part of the story and presented in a manner that even persons completely ignorant of law can benefit and understand them. I simply fell in love with this book and Theo's style in clarifying the problems to his friends.  In my opinion, this is the way a complicated subject should be introduced to children.  This book should have a place in every school library and be prescribed as an additional reading in the middle and high school.  Because the novel is written for the young readers, the language is simple, the narration is smooth, and the contents are not only interesting but also educational.  Some have criticized this book, saying that the plot, theme, and the characters are flat.  When the kid starts explaining the meaning of the words related to law, the critics say children will fall asleep because it is boring.  I am afraid that is not my opinion.  I find the characters are lively and the explanations about the words pertaining to law are clear. The critics also feel a 13 years old boy cannot comprehend law so well and it is rather unrealistic.  I fully disagree with the criticism.  Children's minds are like sponges and absorb much more than we think of them. Children take whatever we offer when they are growing.    The only criticism that I have is, the ending is vague.  Mr. Grisham leaves it to the reader's imagination although he makes it clear that the judge believes the witness has told the truth.

0312600844|5.0|Few writers offer such intense emotional journeys with their characters as Franzen.  His patient writing pays off in building sturdy psychological profiles with his characters. It was worth the nine years in waiting for the follow-up to the highly publicized Corrections.

B002BSHXJA|3.0|Having had the time now to compare both the 'stereo' and mono releases of the Beatles catalog, I can safely say, I wished they were better.    My first real complaint is with the media as a whole, be it video or audio. The buzz word 'remaster' usually makes a person 'sweat with anticipation', and gets them to think that 'the ultimate version of (fill in the blank) has finally arrived. But there's several things to consider too.    Usually what I've found with the word 'remaster' of any product I've purchased is simply this - and this alone,' it just means 'they did something to it.' (Whoever 'they' may be.) So now we come to the Beatles catalog.     Are they 'remastered?' Yes, the stereo versions have been 'tinkered with,' no question about that. Are the mono versions 'remastered?' That's questionable.     The main thing about both of these sets is this. They are new transfers. And yes, they are better then the '87 releases - noticeably.    But other than the 'stereo' versions being better transfers, and of course they did some EQ work on them, as well as added some limiting to make them louder, I can't call either one of these box sets 'the ultimate.'     The new stereo versions are indeed better transfers then the previous releases as well as the mono versions. This is the unifying likeness of box sets.     There are many good observations by costumers on here already. The 'Cheapskates Guide' is very well thought out and put together. I have seen many other reviews here as well that anyone considering purchasing one of these sets should read. One memorable review I read that the listener noticed 'a lot of 'artifacts' as he put it. He is absolutely correct.     As I read some of the interviews with the audio engineers, one of their comments was 'we did very little to the tracks, if anything.' And the truth is I would believe his comment after careful listening. In one of the interviews I read one of the engineers commented about using some minor 'editing' to cleanup a 'hard low end plosive' (pop), but again they mentioned they did not do that much to the new transfers.     Personally, I'm very surprised at what the audio guys did NOT do. And yep, there's a lot of 'dirt' that could be 'righteously cleaned' to improve the tracks. BUT ... there in itself is a hot button (so to speak) with people. And that in itself whenever you 'remotely hint' at anything resembling the 'dreaded monster of 'noise reduction' people usually panic. So someone made the decision to use very little - if any at all on these new releases. (If I recall correctly, only about 5 minutes total for the entire catalog, and even that was no more than minor spots that I'm more than sure it was bad enough 'something had to be done.')    I personally don't think they did much of anything to the mono set. And right out of the gate for me there are tons of things I can hear that tell me this. Yep, like one reviewer said, 'lots of 'artifacts.' there is plenty of pops left, and lots of 'crackle' too. Most noticeably to me the 'graininess of the vocals' at times. So you 'die hard purests' that are 'afraid they did something' to 'your Beatles' mono set - fear not. And if they did, I can assuredly tell you since I own them - I sure wish they had done a lot more.     A good reviewer mentioned that the mono set is 'bottom heavy.' He is correct too. They are. Sometimes it's great for that particular track though. Other times it's just too much and makes the track 'muddy sounding.' Sure, it does add that 'bottom end punch', and man that can make a big difference to the right track. But with a track that is predominately 'acoustic', too much is just too much. (Rubber Soul comes to mind) But then you're faced with some 'gawd awful fake stereo' on the 'stereo version' of that CD. If you don't stand in the middle of the stereo field, it's tolerable, but if you do, it just nothing but annoying with that 'hard pan left / hard pan right' non-sense. (Personal preference noted here- of course).    As a whole though, I can say I do prefer the mono set over the stereo. But I say this with 'some' reservation too. The stereo mix of Penny Lane (which has always been a favorite of mine), is more pleasant to listen to then the mono version. So at best, I can't tell anyone 'one set' is better then the other, as in 'stereo vs. mono.' To me it's a 'mixed' bag, but again, I lean toward the mono set.     I can't give these set a 'glowing 'recommendation.' If you want a better then you got (as in the '87 releases), you will get that sonically.     But if you want something as absolutely wonderful as the REAL REMASTERS of 'Yellow Submarine' and/or the 'Love' CD - you won't find them in these sets. George & Giles Martin (and Geoff Emerick too I think), did an amazing job with the Yellow Submarine CD that was released about the same time as when the movie was re-released. (Which by the way does constitute for a 'real remaster' of a movie too.) The 'Love' CD, won a Grammy Award, so I'm not the only one to applaud the work done on that one.    So, my complaint only with the EMI, APPLE ... OR ANY OTHER COMPANY that claims 'remaster' - be honest enough to tell people - yeah we did something to it ... but they are still dirty looking ... dirty sounding ... and never got any PRISTINE RESTORATION WORK done on them. Oh sure, we EQ'd them. Oh sure, we made them louder, oh sure we made the picture darker, oh sure we fiddled with the sound ....     But to call it a 'remaster' ... if it was a bad track to begin with .. or a bad print that needs RESTORATION done to it ... just doesn't cut it at all with me anymore.     Which bring me to a close on this. Except for one final point. I have no idea why Apple said 'it took four years' to get these box sets out. The artwork? The legal hassles? I don't know. But to me when a audio engineer tells me 'they did very little to the tracks' and I can tell they didn't, why promote them as such, as if to imply the entire audio team spent that kind of time making these possible. (?) If Apple says that, then you gave the audio guys some very very long tea breaks, (Since Abbey Road studios in England.)     At any rate, I do lean toward the mono's on several things, but even those tracks could have been much better.     I have no doubt - friends and neighbors - we have not seen the last of the phrase 'newly remastered' ... and I sure hope Giles Martian gets on board here, even if he can't get his dad out of retirement.     Help us 'Obi Wan Martin' - you're our only hope! (And please bring Geoff Skywalker with you !) What we REALLY WANT is to see Apple REMIX ANYTHING THEY CAN .. with whatever is left ... from the master tapes. AND to do that 'righteously' ... ALL the master tapes need FULL RESTORATION work done on them. (Did I YELL THAT LOUD ENOUGH! ... (hehe!)    Am I glad I got the mono set, sure. But again, I wished they'd been better.    Take care all, and enjoy what we got for now.

006145205X|1.0|In adding my voice to the critics of The Rational Optimist\\" I confine myself to some topics covered in the book which relate to my expertise and not addressed by other reviewers.     The categorical statement: \\"The inescapable fact is that most technological change comes from attempts to improve existing technologies.\\" pg.258 is difficult to square with the dominant source of innovation, industrial research, in the 19th and 20th century in close collaboration with major research universities.    There is no basis for Ridley's statement \\"The great innovators are still usually the outsiders.\\" pg. 260  The semiconductor industry which has been the backbone of the burgeoning world of information and communication is based upon the inventions from  a few major industrial labs such as Bell, IBM, NEC and Intel.     Nor is the statement \\" ... government spending on R & D has no observable effect on economic growth\\"  supported by the evidence of the last hundred years and the increase in such spending the   world over. One need only think of nuclear energy, the internet and the genome effort, hardly unknown to Ridley.     These are a few examples of some of the sweeping conclusions which have no basis in fact. They show an arrogance on the part of a successful and respected author, that he can  get away with anything and a willingness of a publisher to go along with it.       Anyone interested in this aspect of Ridley's distorted presentation can turn to the balanced and well researched exposition by Robert Buderi : Engines of Tomorrow, Simon & Schuster, 2000 rather than Ridley's source, Terence Kealey's, Sex, Science and Profits, William Heinemann  Ltd. 2008.

B000002SE0|5.0|I believe there are two definitive studio recordings of Turandot in the market; in no particular order you can choose either from the Sutherland/Pavarotti/Mehta reading or this one with Nilsson/Corelli/Molinari-Pradelli. So, which one should you choose?, well I'll describe some pro's and con's of both recordings before my final recommendation.    First of all, Birgit Nilsson IS Turandot. When she begins her singing in Act II you can really hear and feel the ice hearted princess. Her phrasing and attention to dramatic accents in \\"In questa reggia\\" are thrilling. Seas of ink have been written about her clear, brilliant and powerful high notes which she displays with full splendor during the recording. However, once Calaf has given her the melting kiss of love, her voice transforms immediately into a vulnerable woman in love. Her dramatic understanding of the role, as well as her vocal fireworks are fully demonstrated in this recording. On the competing set we have Joan Sutherland as Turandot who, although great in dramatic lyric roles such as Norma or Lucia, falls short as Turandot. She just doesn't has Nilsson's commanding power and icy vocal color. Joan sings the part beautifully (almost too beautifully for the what this raging role requires) but her voice does not catch enough drama during the most intense scenes. For the Turandot part, Nilsson comes victorious over Joan.    Franco Corelli is the incarnation of Calaf. It is true that Corelli was much more \\"on fire\\" on the stage than in studio, in this recording he shows his beautiful, masculine timbre and displays incredible breath control, however he lacks some brilliance in the high register as well as the careful, disciplined phrasing that he used to show in live recordings. None the less, his \\"Non piangere, Liu\\" is the best rendition of the aria on studio, ending the scene with the third \\"Turandot\\" call lasting forever. Corelli was the only tenor with the vocal authority and exploding accents to make you reallyfeel that the prince is fighting a match for his life against the icy princess. His trademark moments such as the high C in the phrase \\"Ti voglio ardente d'amor\\" are there, which he hits with one breath in an incredible display of technique. He is, hands down, the most vibrant, powerful and energized Calaf on record. On the competitors set we have Luciano Pavarotti as Calaf, creating on of his best recordings on studio. He has what Corelli lacks which is a beautiful phrasing in absolutely all the role's lines, as well as fresh brilliant notes. Needless to say that his \\"Nessun dorma\\" is a benchmark. However, he sounds miniature if you compare him with Corelli's titanic powers. Pavarotti is more refined and careful with the role's details, but Corelli is electryfing and wild in his interpretation. So, who emerges winner?, I believe this is a tie...with Pavarotti you have waves of beautiful sounds one after the other but miss a bit the sensation of a strong hearted prince, with Corelli you have a more sloppy phrasing but passionate singing full of power, fire and excitement.    Molinari-Pradelli conducts the orchestra with brilliance and clarity but has nothing to do against Zubin Mehta's tempestuous and exciting reading of the score which, hands down, is THE best reading on studio in the market.    So, which is the best Turandot on studio?...well there is no clear first or second place, as everything in Opera, it is a matter of your own taste...my suggestion is; if you want the greatest princess ever recorded, paired by the most powerful and dramatic (if not quite beautifully phrased) prince, then go for Nilsson/Corelli. If you want a less dramatic experience but filled with beautful sounds from both the main characters, go for Sutherland/Pavarotti.    I can't live without both!

I was seriously sitting there crying as the film quickly went through the motions of the man and woman starting out as children, then growing up and getting married, fixing up their house, not being able to have a baby, growing old, and eventually leading to the wife departing. Upon landing in South America, the old man and the boy scout meet up with some animals and have the adventure of a lifetime.      Up is a really good animated film for anyone looking to spend a little entertainment with their family.  I highly recommend it. B004FCEFUK|5.0|Let me just start by saying that I've found a truism in music of all genres over time: the \\"catchiest\\" music usually is the most quickly appreciated, but it's also the quickest to fade from memory. This truism challenged my appreciation of The Great Escape at first. Let me explain.     I was expecting another album like Mercy Falls.  Frankly, I think Mercy Falls is one of the most ingenious albums ever.  I think the story is good, and the arrangement and melodies are simply extraordinary. The album was breathtaking, memorable, melodious, and powerful. Said another way, Mercy Falls was Seventh Wonder's \\"Scenes from a Memory\\", but frankly they did it better than Dream Theater ever could.  I most appreciate memorable melodies, where Mercy Falls excels.    Enter The Great Escape. Frankly on first listen, all I heard was a bunch of random pieces strung together, most that weren't so memorable at first.  I KNEW I had to keep listening to it, and sure enough, my above truism came to fruition: I started to pick apart the album and realize what it was - a stroke of genius. It took a good dozen repeats and devoted listening.  Stick with it and you'll probably get the same reaction.    With an album that is perhaps far more intense and even mature than Mercy Falls, what the Great Escape lacks in Mercy Fall's memorable melodies makes up in deeper, more complex, more layered vocals and music. Where Seventh Wonder came of age with Mercy Falls, they achieved mastery with The Great Escape.    While all the songs are great, the title track The Great Escape, at like 25 minutes, is astounding.  This was the song I understood least upon the first 5-10 listens, but it is now the one I prefer most. What finally matured my understanding of that song was two things: finding out what Aniara, the basis for the whole song, was; and reading the lyrics along with the song.    First, you need to know about Aniara to make sense of the song.  [...]     Apparently, Aniara was the only piece of science fiction that ever won the Noble Prize. (Although Frank Herbert should've won it for Dune, but that's another essay...) In short, it's an epic poem from the 1950s about the escape of a group of refugees aboard a ship from Earth to Mars.  Their ship, Aniara, is hit by an asteroid and, having lost navigation, subsequently drifts away out of the solar system on an endless voyage into a dark abyss. The refugees look to a computer on the ship, Mima, who can create lifelike environments for them that remind them of home (sort of like Star Trek's holo-deck), for respite from their awful predicament. However, Mima, seeing how terribly the humans are treating themselves and each other, pulls the plug on herself.  Out of desperation and depression from their sense of hopelessness, they fall into a variety of rituals, sacrifices, lust-ridden ceremonies, and finally accept their fate and eventually (I think) all die after 24 years. It's a highly intense, emotional, and tragic story with a lot of humanity in it.      The Great Escape is the re-telling of this story, with the same storytelling ability and passion. In fact, sitting with the lyrics in front of me, I swear I had to fight back tears in a couple parts... all I could think was, \\"What am I doing, I'm a grown man practically tear-struck by some music?\\"  It's done really well, with good and genuine lyrics, and the backing music supports the story well.  The big point here is that to appreciate the whole thing, you need to sit through it like a movie and listen to it.  If you flick it on in your car for 10 minutes at a time, you're going to miss the experience, and the music will seem unmemorable.  It's when you piece the whole thing together with the story that you find out this is an incredible piece of art.    Aside from what I said above about the story, the music throughout the album is just great.  The album production is great too.  All in all, this is an extraordinary production.    I DO have one complaint, buti didn't want to ding the album for it.  There are a number of melodies on the album that are almost direct copies of the main melody from Mercy Falls.  I hate to say it, but it's pretty obvious that The Great Escape was leftover material from Mercy Falls.  They probably recorded it and didn't it use for that album, so they reused it for this one. I don't think it's a huge deal, especially given how different this album's content is, but it's pretty noticeable.    In summary, if you're looking for something new to listen to and immerse yourself in something that also tells a great story, pick this album up.  Get Mercy Falls too if you haven't listened to it.  These guys are definitely at the top of their genre - which is simply incredible for a group of part-time musicians. I suspect the guys from Dream Theater, Circus Maximus, Kamelot, and others will be listening to Seventh Wonder's albums to learn something.
B00008I8IF|4.0|Comfort music may seem like a bit of a backhanded compliment for this one, but trust me, it's not. I enjoyed the material as an informative refresher as well as updating the knowledge gained during completion of my Bachelor of Science in biological sciences. Dawkins does a wonderful job, at times in witty ways, of informing the listener of our current understandings of how and why change occurs in species over time.   There are a few places that I felt it would have been helpful to have the book in visual form, strictly from the standpoint of comprehending Dawkin's extensive vocabulary. Access to references to further investigate additional resources on the subjects covered by the book is another encouraging reason to have it in visual form.  The fascinating material and Dawkin's style have prompted me to enjoy the book more than once.    (The reviewer was compensated for posting this review. In this book, none of the characters seemed to have grown up or changed and the characters that did changed changed in horrible ways. 081297381X|2.0|That black swans (and their gray brethren) exist is almost indisputable.  Unfortunately Taleb spoils this idea with a few hundred additional pages of nonsensical prose.  He expounds on the \\"narrative fallacy,\\" and falls victim to it (perhaps ironically--perhaps intentionally) with made up stories about a made up author.  He offers up the trappings of epistemic arrogance, and likewise seems to fall victim to them.  He expresses his profound distaste for statistics, and he seems to avoid entertaining the potential of his own arrogance.    Bottom line: the overall idea of black swans is a worthy essay.  Taleb's book is overdone, full of hubris, and unnecessarily critical of just about everything and everybody.  He presents some interesting ideas about our many psychological trappings as humans--then seems to fall victim to them himself.

In person.<br />Sam, ever since he was bitten by a wolf as a boy, is a werewolf. During the hot temperature of summer he is human, but during the frigid cold of winter, he transforms into a wolf. But this year is Sam's last year of being human, and as the winter comes closer and closer, the young couple must fight to prevent him from turning back into a wolf. Even if it means risking their lives.<br /><br />I didn't really know what to expect for this book. Besides the Twilight saga, this is the only other werewolf book I've ever read. Maggie Stiefvater's writing was beautifully written. She detailed every paragraph, easily letting me visualize the story in my head.<br /><br />The novel is told through both Grace and Sam's perspective. But what I loved about the book was that both of their characters were so distinctive, that I was able to tell who was telling the story even without reading the chapter titles (which told which character's POV it was in.) She stood up for herself and didn't take no for an answer. Sam and Grace complemented each other, and were made for each other. Their love was refreshing, and the author wrote it very descriptively. B003EY7IRC|4.0|I am a long-time fan of Karen's, but I will not, just because I enjoy an author, give false praise where it does not belong. So, that said, this review will be honest, direct, and will tell you what I genuinely thought, good and bad, even at the risk (without intent) of offending Karen (whom I've actually even met along with her husband).  So, here it goes:    Shadowfever cannot be reviewed in a vacuum because it is the culmination of a 5-book series and must be reviewed in that context. Shadowfever was much better than I expected because I was honestly disappointed by Dreamfever, the 4th book in the series (which I also reviewed here - and the fact that Mac & Barron had a lot of sex in that book could not overcome what I felt it was lacking in plot development).     I LOVED the 1st three books in the series (Darkfever, Bloodfever & Faefever). After it got going past a slow start, Shadowfever definitely held my rapt attention right to the end. It had intriguing plot twists that no one had guessed. And shocking, sometimes quite abrupt surprises. I would still say that Dreamfever and the first portion of this book contained a lot of unnecessary and sometimes annoying filler. The series would have been better cut down to four action-packed, heart-wrenching, gripping books.     But, the series is filled with complex and well-developed characters, who come alive in a vibrant world to live through an excellent plot that becomes quite real for the reader. There are many sides to the characters, good and bad, and many reasons why some do bad for good reasons or good for bad reasons.  In the end, it would be hard to pick a single villain or hero from the cast. Shadowfever provides essentially all the critical answers people will need for closure (except a few details and technically \\"what\\" Barrons is). It ends with a few things left in the air to allow for new plots and a continuing storyline, but not a cliffhanger. So, this is really not an end to stories in the Fever Worldor with these characters. But, do the fans really want that anyway?     Shadowfever (except the first approx. 20%) moved well and provided substantial closure. My main issue with Shadowfever and with the series as a whole was that it never provided the development of the relationship between the main characters. Some folks are just so desperate to have an HEA (happily ever after), that as long as the two presumed lovers end up together and/or have sex, they're happy. But, I require more or I'd rather there not even be an HEA. It needs to develop enough to make sense.  There really was no transition or reason for the change of emotional development for either of the main characters. I hate you, I treat you like crap, I fight with you, We have sex, You're my whole life, I will die without you. That was a jump on the intellectual level of a playground romance. I kick you therefor I love you. I needed far more to make the leap with them.     Overall, I had closure with this book, but the relationship was not fulfilling for me at all. That is why I cannot give this book 5 stars. It never tied up the loose ends of what was totally lacking in the relationship throughout this series and remains lacking to the end. However, I will read the next book Karen writes, as I've read everything else.    P.S. As an overall comment on all the books in the series, including this one: You may or may not like the way it is written from the 1st person point of view at times. When some of the characters get whiny or emotional, if you would not like that in person, it may annoy you to read. It does immerse you in the character and their perspective. But, depending on your personality, you may want to tell them to \\"shut up\\"! So, that is the risk the author chose to take.

0399226907|5.0|I remember the first time my kindergarten teacher read The Very Hungry Caterpillar to the class. We were all fascinated by this colorful book about a caterpillar who eats his way through a lot of food, then becomes a butterfly. Until the last couple of months, I hadn't picked up the book since 1990. But when I saw it at the store, I got it off the shelf to read to my daughter, and see if she liked it as much as I did then. Sure enough, she did. This is just a charming little book. It might not be much of a story book for bed time or anything, but it's definitely a neat one to look at.<br /><br />The book is just a few pages long, but it tells the short story about a hungry little caterpillar who isn't satisfied when he eats a lot of fruit, so he moves on to snacks and what most people consider junk food, which makes him huge. The caterpillar then makes its cocoon, and emerges as a beautiful butterfly.<br /><br />This book is for very young children, as you can see by the reading level in the product details here. It's best for babies to pre-schoolers, though it was quite fine with a kindergarten class too. Unless you're in an area that has a lot of caterpillars and butterflies, some kids aren't exposed to them much or know that caterpillars tun into butterflies, so this is a great book to show that. And like I stated above, the artwork is fantastic. Eric Carle has a very distinct style, going with tissue paper rather than pencil, ink or paint. Simple things like a hot dog or plum look great in their own right with this style, and even now as a hopeful children's book author, I admire this artwork. I also love how the caterpillar shows no emotion, except for when its eaten too much and you can tell he has a pretty bad tummy ache.<br /><br />But there is one thing that's throwing off a lot of people who order this book, and it's the same thing that happened to me with The Very Busy Spider. Only in the product details do you see the size of the book listed. Most peoplewould assume that these books are your more regular book size, rather than a mini version that fits in your back pocket. Sometimes the edition of the book isn't mentioned in the item title either, so please read the product details before placing an order so you don't end up disappointed when a tiny book arrives.<br /><br />The Very Hungry Caterpillar is a book that most children will enjoy, and is a nice one to look at in the car, grocery shopping, or as a prep for a bigger bedtime book. Eric Carle just has that magic touch of making simple, short stories that are memorable for years to come...with a big help from colorful tissue paper!
